class="firstHeading" lang="en">Golf</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a (disambiguation)">Golfer (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n\n</p>\n<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><caption class="fn" style="text-align:center;background:#eee;">Presence</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.2em;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:1.0em;"><a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic</a></th><td style="line-height:1.3em;">\n<a href="/wiki/1900_Summer_Olympics" title="1900 Summer Olympics">1900</a>, <a href="/wiki/1904_Summer_Olympics" title="1904 Summer Olympics">1904</a>, <a href="/wiki/2016_Summer_Olympics" title="2016 Summer Olympics">2016</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/2020_Summer_Olympics" title="2020 Summer Olympics">2020</a><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Golf</b> is a club-and-ball <a href="/wiki/Sport" title="Sport">sport</a> in which players use various <a href="/wiki/Golf_club" title="Golf club">clubs</a> to hit <a href="/wiki/Golf_ball" title="Golf ball">balls</a> into a series of holes on a <a href="/wiki/Golf_course" title="Golf course">course</a> in as few strokes as possible.\n</p><p>Golf, unlike most <a href="/wiki/Ball_game" title="Ball game">ball games</a>, cannot and does not utilize a standardized playing area, and coping with the varied terrains encountered on different courses is a key part of the game. The game at the usual level is played on a course with an arranged progression of 18 holes, though recreational courses can be smaller, often having 9 holes. Each hole on the course must contain a <a href="/wiki/Teeing_ground" title="Teeing ground">tee box</a> to start from, and a <a href="/wiki/Putting_green" class="mw-redirect" title="Putting green">putting green</a> containing the actual hole or cup (4.25 inches in diameter). There are other standard forms of terrain in between, such as the fairway, rough (long grass), bunkers (or "sand traps"), and various hazards (water, rocks) but each hole on a course is unique in its specific layout and arrangement.\n</p><p>Golf is played for the lowest number of strokes by an individual, known as <a href="/wiki/Stroke_play" title="Stroke play">stroke play</a>, or the lowest score on the most individual holes in a complete round by an individual or team, known as <a href="/wiki/Match_play" title="Match play">match play</a>. Stroke play is the most commonly seen format at all levels, but most especially at the elite level.\n</p><p>The modern game of golf <a href="/wiki/Golf_in_Scotland" title="Golf in Scotland">originated in 15th century Scotland</a>. The 18-hole round was created at the <a href="/wiki/Old_Course_at_St_Andrews" title="Old Course at St Andrews">Old Course</a> at <a href="/wiki/St_Andrews" title="St Andrews">St Andrews</a> in 1764. Golf\'s first major, and the world\'s oldest tournament in existence, is <a href="/wiki/The_Open_Championship" title="The Open Championship">The Open Championship</a>, also known as the British Open, which was first played in 1860 in Ayrshire, Scotland. This is one of the four <a href="/wiki/Men%27s_major_golf_championships" title="Men&#39;s major golf championships">major championships</a> in men\'s professional golf, the other three being played in the United States: <a href="/wiki/The_Masters" class="mw-redirect" title="The Masters">The Masters</a>, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Open_(golf)" title="U.S. Open (golf)">U.S. Open</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/PGA_Championship" title="PGA Championship">PGA Championship</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_golf" title="History of golf">History of golf</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_MacDonald_boys_playing_golf.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/The_MacDonald_boys_playing_golf.jpg/220px-The_MacDonald_boys_playing_golf.jpg" width="220" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/The_MacDonald_boys_playing_golf.jpg/330px-The_MacDonald_boys_playing_golf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/The_MacDonald_boys_playing_golf.jpg/440px-The_MacDonald_boys_playing_golf.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1449" data-file-height="1769" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_MacDonald_boys_playing_golf.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The MacDonald boys playing golf</i>, attributed to William Mosman. 18th century, <a href="/wiki/National_Galleries_of_Scotland" title="National Galleries of Scotland">National Galleries of Scotland</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>While the modern game of golf originated in 15th-century Scotland, the game\'s ancient origins are unclear and much debated. Some historians<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> trace the sport back to the Roman game of <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/paganica" class="extiw" title="wikt:paganica">paganica</a>, in which participants used a bent stick to hit a stuffed leather ball. One theory asserts that paganica spread throughout Europe as the Romans conquered most of the continent, during the first century BC, and eventually evolved into the modern game.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Others cite <a href="/wiki/Chuiwan" title="Chuiwan">chuiwan</a> ("chui" means striking and "wan" means small ball) as the progenitor, a Chinese game played between the eighth and fourteenth centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> A Ming Dynasty scroll dating back to 1368 entitled "The Autumn Banquet" shows a member of the Chinese Imperial court swinging what appears to be a golf club at a small ball with the aim of sinking it into a hole. The game is thought to have been introduced into Europe during the Middle Ages. Another early game that resembled modern golf was known as <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cambuca" class="extiw" title="wikt:cambuca">cambuca</a> in England and chambot in France.<sup id="cite_ref-TUGB54_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TUGB54-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The Persian game <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/chaug%C3%A1n" class="extiw" title="wikt:chaug\xc3\xa1n">chaug\xc3\xa1n</a> is another possible ancient origin. In addition, <a href="/wiki/Kolven" title="Kolven">kolven</a> (a game involving a ball and curved bats) was played annually in Loenen, Netherlands, beginning in 1297, to commemorate the capture of the assassin of <a href="/wiki/Floris_V,_Count_of_Holland" title="Floris V, Count of Holland">Floris V</a>, a year earlier.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Four_gentlemen_golfers_on_the_tee_of_a_golf_course_(5014828806).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Four_gentlemen_golfers_on_the_tee_of_a_golf_course_%285014828806%29.jpg/220px-Four_gentlemen_golfers_on_the_tee_of_a_golf_course_%285014828806%29.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Four_gentlemen_golfers_on_the_tee_of_a_golf_course_%285014828806%29.jpg/330px-Four_gentlemen_golfers_on_the_tee_of_a_golf_course_%285014828806%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Four_gentlemen_golfers_on_the_tee_of_a_golf_course_%285014828806%29.jpg/440px-Four_gentlemen_golfers_on_the_tee_of_a_golf_course_%285014828806%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="534" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Four_gentlemen_golfers_on_the_tee_of_a_golf_course_(5014828806).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Four gentlemen golfers on the tee of a golf course, 1930s</div></div></div>\n<p>The modern game <a href="/wiki/Golf_in_Scotland" title="Golf in Scotland">originated in Scotland</a>, where the first written record of golf is <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_Scotland" title="James II of Scotland">James II</a>\'s banning of the game in 1457, as an unwelcome distraction to learning archery.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/King_James_IV_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="King James IV of Scotland">James IV</a> lifted the ban in 1502 when he became a golfer himself, with golf clubs first recorded in 1503\xe2\x80\x931504: "For golf clubbes and balles to the King that he playit with".<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> To many golfers, the <a href="/wiki/Old_Course_at_St_Andrews" title="Old Course at St Andrews">Old Course</a> at <a href="/wiki/St_Andrews" title="St Andrews">St Andrews</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Links_(golf)" title="Links (golf)">links course</a> dating to before 1574, is considered to be a site of pilgrimage.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In 1764, the standard 18-hole golf course was created at St Andrews when members modified the course from 22 to 18 holes.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Golf is documented as being played on <a href="/wiki/Musselburgh_Links" title="Musselburgh Links">Musselburgh Links</a>, East Lothian, Scotland as early as 2 March 1672, which is certified as the oldest golf course in the world by Guinness World Records.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The oldest surviving <a href="/wiki/Rules_of_golf" title="Rules of golf">rules of golf</a> were compiled in March 1744 for the Company of Gentlemen Golfers, later renamed <a href="/wiki/The_Honourable_Company_of_Edinburgh_Golfers" class="mw-redirect" title="The Honourable Company of Edinburgh Golfers">The Honourable Company of Edinburgh Golfers</a>, which was played at <a href="/wiki/Leith_Links" title="Leith Links">Leith</a>, Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The world\'s oldest golf tournament in existence, and golf\'s first major, is <a href="/wiki/The_Open_Championship" title="The Open Championship">The Open Championship</a>, which was first played on 17 October 1860 at Prestwick Golf Club, in Ayrshire, Scotland, with <a href="/wiki/Men%27s_major_golf_championships#Major_championship_winners" title="Men&#39;s major golf championships">Scottish golfers winning the earliest majors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Two Scotsmen from <a href="/wiki/Dunfermline" title="Dunfermline">Dunfermline</a>, John Reid and Robert Lockhart, first demonstrated golf in the U.S. by setting up a hole in an orchard in 1888, with Reid setting up America\'s first golf club the same year, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Andrew%27s_Golf_Club" title="Saint Andrew&#39;s Golf Club">Saint Andrew\'s Golf Club</a> in Yonkers, New York.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Golf_course">Golf course</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Golf_course_Golfplatz_Wittenbeck_Mecklenburg_Ostsee_Baltic_Sea_Germany.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Golf_course_Golfplatz_Wittenbeck_Mecklenburg_Ostsee_Baltic_Sea_Germany.jpg/220px-Golf_course_Golfplatz_Wittenbeck_Mecklenburg_Ostsee_Baltic_Sea_Germany.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Golf_course_Golfplatz_Wittenbeck_Mecklenburg_Ostsee_Baltic_Sea_Germany.jpg/330px-Golf_course_Golfplatz_Wittenbeck_Mecklenburg_Ostsee_Baltic_Sea_Germany.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Golf_course_Golfplatz_Wittenbeck_Mecklenburg_Ostsee_Baltic_Sea_Germany.jpg/440px-Golf_course_Golfplatz_Wittenbeck_Mecklenburg_Ostsee_Baltic_Sea_Germany.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="685" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Golf_course_Golfplatz_Wittenbeck_Mecklenburg_Ostsee_Baltic_Sea_Germany.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial view of the <i>Golfplatz <a href="/wiki/Wittenbeck" title="Wittenbeck">Wittenbeck</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Mecklenburg" title="Mecklenburg">Mecklenburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Golf_course" title="Golf course">Golf course</a></div>\n<p>A golf course consists of either 9 or 18 holes, each with a <a href="/wiki/Teeing_ground" title="Teeing ground">teeing ground</a> that is set off by two markers showing the bounds of the legal tee area, <a href="/wiki/Fairway_(golf)#Fairway_and_rough" class="mw-redirect" title="Fairway (golf)">fairway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fairway_(golf)#Fairway_and_rough" class="mw-redirect" title="Fairway (golf)">rough</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Hazard_(golf)" title="Hazard (golf)">hazards</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Putting_green" class="mw-redirect" title="Putting green">putting green</a> surrounded by the fringe with the pin (normally a flagstick) and cup.\n</p><p>The levels of grass are varied to increase difficulty, or to allow for putting in the case of the green. While many holes are designed with a direct line-of-sight from the teeing area to the green, some holes may bend either to the left or to the right. This is commonly called a "dogleg", in reference to a dog\'s knee. The hole is called a "dogleg left" if the hole angles leftwards and "dogleg right" if it bends right. Sometimes, a hole\'s direction may bend twice; this is called a "double dogleg".\n</p><p>A regular golf course consists of 18 holes, but nine-hole courses are common and can be played twice through for a full round of 18 holes.<sup id="cite_ref-Encarta_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encarta-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Early Scottish golf courses were primarily laid out on links land, soil-covered sand dunes directly inland from beaches.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> This gave rise to the term "golf links", particularly applied to seaside courses and those built on naturally sandy soil inland.\n</p><p>The first 18-hole golf course in the United States was on a sheep farm in <a href="/wiki/Downers_Grove,_Illinois" title="Downers Grove, Illinois">Downers Grove, Illinois</a>, in 1892. The course is still there today.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Play_of_the_game">Play of the game</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Golf_field.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Golf_field.svg/170px-Golf_field.svg.png" width="170" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Golf_field.svg/255px-Golf_field.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Golf_field.svg/340px-Golf_field.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="420" data-file-height="583" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Golf_field.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1=teeing ground, 2=water hazard, 3=rough, 4=out of bounds, 5=<a href="/wiki/Hazard_(golf)#Bunker" title="Hazard (golf)">sand bunker</a>, 6=water hazard, 7=fairway, 8=putting green, 9=flagstick, 10=hole</div></div></div>\n<p>Every round of golf is based on playing a number of holes in a given order. A "round" typically consists of 18 holes that are played in the order determined by the course layout. Each hole is played once in the round on a standard course of 18 holes. The game can be played by any number of people. Though a typical group playing will have 1-4 people playing the round. The typical amount of time required for pace of play for a 9-hole round is two hours and four hours for an 18-hole round.\n</p><p>Playing a hole on a golf course is initiated by putting a ball into play by striking it with a club on the <a href="/wiki/Teeing_ground" title="Teeing ground">teeing ground</a> (also called the tee box, or simply the tee). For this first shot on each hole, it is allowed but not required for the golfer to place the ball on a <a href="/wiki/Tee#Golf_tee" title="Tee">tee</a> prior to striking it. A tee is a small peg that can be used to elevate the ball slightly above the ground up to a few centimetres high. Tees are commonly made of wood but may be constructed of any material, including plastic. Traditionally, golfers used mounds of sand to elevate the ball, and containers of sand were provided for the purpose. A few courses still require sand to be used instead of peg tees, to reduce litter and reduce damage to the teeing ground. Tees help reduce the interference of the ground or grass on the movement of the club making the ball easier to hit, and also places the ball in the very centre of the striking face of the club (the "sweet spot") for better distance.\n</p><p>When the initial shot on a hole is intended to move the ball a long distance (typically more than 225 yards (210&#160;m)), the shot is commonly called a "drive" and is generally made with a long-shafted, large-headed <a href="/wiki/Wood_(golf)" title="Wood (golf)">wood</a> club called a "driver". Shorter holes may be initiated with other clubs, such as higher-numbered woods or <a href="/wiki/Iron_(golf)" title="Iron (golf)">irons</a>. Once the ball comes to rest, the golfer strikes it again as many times as necessary using shots that are variously known as a "lay-up", an "approach", a "pitch", or a "<a href="/wiki/Golf_stroke_mechanics#Chip" title="Golf stroke mechanics">chip</a>", until the ball reaches the green, where he or she then "<a href="/wiki/Putt" class="mw-redirect" title="Putt">putts</a>" the ball into the hole (commonly called "sinking the putt" or "holing out"). The goal of getting the ball into the hole ("holing" the ball) in as few strokes as possible may be impeded by obstacles such as areas of longer grass called "rough" (usually found alongside fairways), which both slows any ball that contacts it and makes it harder to advance a ball that has stopped on it; "doglegs", which are changes in the direction of the fairway that often require shorter shots to play around them; <a href="/wiki/Hazard_(golf)#Bunker" title="Hazard (golf)">bunkers</a> (or sand traps); and <a href="/wiki/Hazard_(golf)#Water_hazard" title="Hazard (golf)">water hazards</a> such as ponds or streams.<sup id="cite_ref-Encarta_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encarta-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In stroke play competitions played according to <a href="/wiki/Strict_rules" class="mw-redirect" title="Strict rules">strict rules</a>, each player plays his or her ball until it is holed no matter how many strokes that may take. In match play it is acceptable to simply pick up one\'s ball and "surrender the hole" after enough strokes have been made by a player that it is mathematically impossible for the player to win the hole. It is also acceptable in informal stroke play to surrender the hole after hitting three strokes more than the "par" rating of the hole (a "triple bogey" - see below); while technically a violation of Rule 3-2, this practice speeds play as a courtesy to others, and avoids "runaway scores", excessive frustration and injuries caused by overexertion.\n</p><p>The total distance from the first tee box to the 18th green can be quite long; total yardages "through the green" can be in excess of 7,000 yards (6,400&#160;m), and when adding in the travel distance between the green of one hole and the tee of the next, even skilled players may easily travel five miles or more during a round. At some courses, electric <a href="/wiki/Golf_carts" class="mw-redirect" title="Golf carts">golf carts</a> are used to travel between shots, which can speed-up play and allows participation by individuals unable to walk a whole round. On other courses players generally walk the course, either carrying their bag using a shoulder strap or using a "golf trolley" for their bag. These trolleys may or may not be battery assisted. At many amateur tournaments including U.S. high school and college play, players are required to walk and to carry their own bags, but at the professional and top amateur level, as well as at high-level private clubs, players may be accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Caddies" class="mw-redirect" title="Caddies">caddies</a>, who carry and manage the players\' equipment and who are allowed by the rules to give advice on the play of the course.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> A caddie\'s advice can only be given to the player or players for whom the caddie is working, and not to other competing players.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rules_and_regulations">Rules and regulations</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rules_of_golf" title="Rules of golf">Rules of golf</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ArnoldPalmerCoastGuard1953.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/ArnoldPalmerCoastGuard1953.jpg/170px-ArnoldPalmerCoastGuard1953.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/ArnoldPalmerCoastGuard1953.jpg/255px-ArnoldPalmerCoastGuard1953.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/ArnoldPalmerCoastGuard1953.jpg/340px-ArnoldPalmerCoastGuard1953.jpg 2x" data-file-width="548" data-file-height="682" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ArnoldPalmerCoastGuard1953.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Arnold_Palmer" title="Arnold Palmer">Arnold Palmer</a> in 1953</div></div></div>\n<p>The rules of golf are internationally standardised and are jointly governed by The R&amp;A, spun off in 2004 from <a href="/wiki/The_Royal_and_Ancient_Golf_Club_of_St_Andrews" title="The Royal and Ancient Golf Club of St Andrews">The Royal and Ancient Golf Club of St Andrews</a> (founded 1754), and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Golf_Association" title="United States Golf Association">United States Golf Association</a> (USGA).<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ROG_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ROG-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The underlying principle of the rules is fairness. As stated on the back cover of the official rule book:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><i>Play the ball as it lies, play the course as you find it, and if you cannot do either, do what is fair.</i></dd></dl>\n<p>There are strict regulations regarding the amateur status of golfers.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Essentially, anybody who has ever received payment or compensation for giving instruction, or played golf for money, is not considered an amateur and may not participate in competitions limited solely to amateurs. However, amateur golfers may receive expenses that comply with strict guidelines and they may accept non-cash prizes within the limits established by the Rules of Amateur Status.\n</p><p>In addition to the officially printed rules, golfers also abide by a set of guidelines called <a href="/wiki/Golf_etiquette" title="Golf etiquette">golf etiquette</a>. Etiquette guidelines cover matters such as safety, fairness, pace of play, and a player\'s obligation to contribute to the care of the course. Though there are no penalties for breach of etiquette rules, players generally follow the rules of golf etiquette in an effort to improve everyone\'s playing experience.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Penalties">Penalties</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Penalty_(golf)" title="Penalty (golf)">Penalty (golf)</a></div>\n<p>Penalties are incurred in certain situations. They are counted towards a player\'s score as if there were extra swing(s) at the ball. Strokes are added for rule infractions or for hitting one\'s ball into an unplayable situation.\n</p><p>A lost ball or a ball hit out of bounds result in a penalty of one stroke and distance (Rule 27\xe2\x80\x931). A one-stroke penalty is assessed if a player\'s equipment causes the ball to move or the removal of a loose impediment causes the ball to move (Rule 18\xe2\x80\x932). A one-stroke penalty is assessed if a player\'s ball results into a red or yellow staked hazard (Rule 26). If a golfer makes a stroke at the wrong ball (Rule 19\xe2\x80\x932) or hits a fellow golfer\'s ball with a putt (Rule 19\xe2\x80\x935), the player incurs a two-stroke penalty. Most rule infractions lead to stroke penalties but also can lead to disqualification. Disqualification could be from cheating, signing for a lower score, or from rule infractions that lead to improper play.<sup id="cite_ref-rules_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rules-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Equipment">Equipment</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Golf_equipment" title="Golf equipment">Golf equipment</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Golf_ball_resting_near_fairway_wood.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Golf_ball_resting_near_fairway_wood.jpg/220px-Golf_ball_resting_near_fairway_wood.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Golf_ball_resting_near_fairway_wood.jpg/330px-Golf_ball_resting_near_fairway_wood.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Golf_ball_resting_near_fairway_wood.jpg/440px-Golf_ball_resting_near_fairway_wood.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="681" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Golf_ball_resting_near_fairway_wood.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Wood_(golf)" title="Wood (golf)">wood</a> positioned ready to be swung and to strike a <a href="/wiki/Golf_ball" title="Golf ball">golf ball</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Golf_club" title="Golf club">Golf clubs</a> are used to hit the <a href="/wiki/Golf_ball" title="Golf ball">golf ball</a>. Each club is composed of a shaft with a lance (or "grip") on the top end and a club head on the bottom. Long clubs, which have a lower amount of degree loft, are those meant to propel the ball a comparatively longer distance, and short clubs a higher degree of loft and a comparatively shorter distance. The actual physical length of each club is longer or shorter, depending on the distance the club is intended to propel the ball.\n</p><p>Golf clubs have traditionally been arranged into three basic types. <a href="/wiki/Wood_(golf)" title="Wood (golf)">Woods</a> are large-headed, long-shafted clubs meant to propel the ball a long distance from relatively "open" lies, such as the tee box and fairway. Of particular importance is the <i>driver</i> or "1-wood", which is the lowest lofted wood club, and in modern times has become highly specialized for making extremely long-distance tee shots, up to 300 yards (270&#160;m), or more, in a professional golfer\'s hands. Traditionally these clubs had heads made of a hardwood, hence the name, but virtually all modern woods are now made of metal such as titanium, or of composite materials. <a href="/wiki/Iron_(golf_club)" class="mw-redirect" title="Iron (golf club)">Irons</a> are shorter-shafted clubs with a metal head primarily consisting of a flat, angled striking face. Traditionally the clubhead was forged from iron; modern iron clubheads are investment-cast from a steel alloy. Irons of varying loft are used for a variety of shots from virtually anywhere on the course, but most often for shorter-distance shots approaching the green, or to get the ball out of tricky lies such as sand traps. The third class is the <a href="/wiki/Putter" title="Putter">putter</a>, which evolved from the irons to create a low-lofted, balanced club designed to roll the ball along the green and into the hole. Putters are virtually always used on the green or in the surrounding rough/fringe. A fourth class, called <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(golf)" title="Hybrid (golf)">hybrids</a>, evolved as a cross between woods and irons, and are typically seen replacing the low-lofted irons with a club that provides similar distance, but a higher launch angle and a more forgiving nature.\n</p><p>A maximum of 14 clubs is allowed in a player\'s bag at one time during a stipulated round. The choice of clubs is at the golfer\'s discretion, although every club must be constructed in accordance with parameters outlined in the rules. (Clubs that meet these parameters are usually called "conforming".) Violation of these rules can result in disqualification.\n</p><p>The exact shot hit at any given time on a golf course, and which club is used to accomplish the shot, are always completely at the discretion of the golfer; in other words, there is no restriction whatsoever on which club a golfer may or may not use at any time for any shot.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Golf_ball" title="Golf ball">Golf balls</a> are spherical, usually white (although other colours are allowed), and minutely pock-marked by dimples that decrease aerodynamic drag by increasing air turbulence around the ball in motion, which delays "boundary layer" separation and reduces the drag-inducing "wake" behind the ball,\nthereby allowing the ball to fly farther.<sup id="cite_ref-ClubHist_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ClubHist-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The combination of a soft "boundary layer" and a hard "core" enables both distance and spin.\n</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Tee#Golf_tee" title="Tee">tee</a> is allowed only for the first stroke on each hole, unless the player must hit a provisional tee shot or replay his or her first shot from the tee.\n</p><p>Many golfers wear <a href="/wiki/Golf_equipment#Shoes" title="Golf equipment">golf shoes</a> with metal or plastic spikes designed to increase traction, thus allowing for longer and more accurate shots.\n</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Golf_bag" class="mw-redirect" title="Golf bag">golf bag</a> is used to transport golf clubs and the player\'s other or personal equipment. Golf bags have several pockets designed for carrying equipment and supplies such as tees, balls, and gloves. Golf bags can be carried, pulled on a trolley or harnessed to a motorized <a href="/wiki/Golf_cart" title="Golf cart">golf cart</a> during play. Golf bags have both a hand strap and shoulder strap for carrying, and sometimes have retractable legs that allow the bag to stand upright when at rest.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Stroke_mechanics">Stroke mechanics</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lady_golfer.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Lady_golfer.jpg/220px-Lady_golfer.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Lady_golfer.jpg/330px-Lady_golfer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Lady_golfer.jpg/440px-Lady_golfer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="418" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lady_golfer.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A golfer takes an approach shot on the fairway.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Golf_stroke_mechanics" title="Golf stroke mechanics">Golf stroke mechanics</a></div>\n<p>The golf swing is outwardly similar to many other motions involving swinging a tool or playing implement, such as an axe or a baseball bat; however, unlike many of these motions, the result of the swing is highly dependent on several sub-motions being properly aligned and timed, to ensure that the club travels up to the ball in line with the desired path, the clubface is in line with the swing path, and the ball hits the centre or "sweet spot" of the clubface. The ability to do this consistently, across a complete set of clubs with a wide range of shaft lengths and clubface areas, is a key skill for any golfer, and takes a significant effort to achieve.\n</p><p>Golfers start with the non-dominant side of the body facing the target (for a right-hander, the target is to their left). At address, the player\'s body and the centerline of the club face are positioned parallel to the desired line of travel, with the feet either perpendicular to that line or slightly splayed outward. The feet are commonly shoulder-width apart for middle irons and putters, narrower for short irons and wider for long irons and woods. The ball is typically positioned more to the "front" of the player\'s stance (closer to the leading foot) for lower-lofted clubs, with the usual ball position for a drive being just behind the arch of the leading foot. The ball is placed further "back" in the player\'s stance (toward the trailing foot) as the loft of the club to be used increases. Most iron shots and putts are made with the ball roughly centered in the stance, while a few mid- and short-iron shots are made with the ball slightly behind the centre of the stance to ensure consistent contact between the ball and clubface, so the ball is on its way before the club continues down into the turf.\n</p><p>The golfer chooses a golf club, grip, and stroke appropriate to the distance:\n</p>\n<ul><li>The "drive" or "full swing" is used on the teeing ground and fairway, typically with a wood or long iron, to produce the maximum distance capable with the club. In the extreme, the windup can end with the shaft of the club parallel to the ground above the player\'s shoulders.</li>\n<li>The "approach" or "3/4 swing" is used in medium- and long-distance situations where an exact distance and good accuracy is preferable to maximum possible distance, such as to place the ball on the green or "lay up" in front of a hazard. The windup or "backswing" of such a shot typically ends up with the shaft of the club pointing straight upwards or slightly towards the player.</li>\n<li>The "chip" or "half-swing" is used for relatively short-distance shots near the green, with high-lofted irons and wedges. The goal of the chip is to land the ball safely on the green, allowing it to roll out towards the hole. It can also be used from other places to accurately position the ball into a more advantageous lie. The backswing typically ends with the head of the club between hip and head height.</li>\n<li>The "putt" is used in short-distance shots on or near the green, typically made with the eponymous "putter", although similar strokes can be made with medium to high-numbered irons to carry a short distance in the air and then roll (a "bump and run"). The backswing and follow-through of the putt are both abbreviated compared to other strokes, with the head of the club rarely rising above the knee. The goal of the putt is usually to put the ball in the hole, although a long-distance putt may be called a "lag" and is made with the primary intention of simply closing distance to the hole or otherwise placing the ball advantageously.</li></ul>\n<p>Having chosen a club and stroke to produce the desired distance, the player addresses the ball by taking their stance to the side of it and (except when the ball lies in a hazard) grounding the club behind the ball. The golfer then takes their backswing, rotating the club, their arms and their upper body away from the ball, and then begins their swing, bringing the clubhead back down and around to hit the ball. A proper golf swing is a complex combination of motions, and slight variations in posture or positioning can make a great deal of difference in how well the ball is hit and how straight it travels. The general goal of a player making a full swing is to propel the clubhead as fast as possible while maintaining a single "plane" of motion of the club and clubhead, to send the clubhead into the ball along the desired path of travel and with the clubhead also pointing that direction.\n</p><p>Accuracy and consistency are typically stressed over pure distance. A player with a straight drive that travels only 220 yards (200&#160;m) will nevertheless be able to accurately place the ball into a favourable lie on the fairway, and can make up for the lesser distance of any given club by simply using "more club" (a lower loft) on their tee shot or on subsequent fairway and approach shots. However, a golfer with a drive that may go 280 yards (260&#160;m) but often doesn\'t fly straight will be less able to position their ball advantageously; the ball may "hook", "pull", "draw", "fade", "push" or "slice" off the intended line and land out of bounds or in the rough or hazards, and thus the player will require many more strokes to hole out.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Musculature">Musculature</span></h3>\n<p>A golf stroke uses muscles on <a href="/wiki/Core_(anatomy)" title="Core (anatomy)">core</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Erector_spinae_muscles" title="Erector spinae muscles">erector spinae muscles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latissimus_dorsi_muscle" title="Latissimus dorsi muscle">latissimus dorsi muscle</a> when turning), <a href="/wiki/Hamstring" title="Hamstring">hamstring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shoulder" title="Shoulder">shoulder</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wrist" title="Wrist">wrist</a>. Stronger muscles on wrist can prevent wrists from being twisted at swings, while stronger shoulders increase the turning force. Weak wrists can also deliver the impacts to elbows and even neck and lead to injury of them. (When a muscle contracts, it pulls equally from both ends and, to have movement at only one end of the muscle, other muscles must come into play to stabilize the bone to which the other end of the muscle is attached.) Golf is a unilateral exercise that can break body balances, requiring exercises to keep the balance in muscles.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_putting">Types of putting</span></h3>\n<p>Putting is considered to be the most important component of the game of golf. As the game of golf has evolved, there have been many different putting techniques and grips that have been devised to give golfers the best chance to make putts. When the game originated, golfers would putt with their dominate hand on the bottom of the grip and their weak hand on top of the grip. This grip and putting style is known as "conventional". There are many variations of conventional including overlap, where the golfer overlaps the off hand index finger onto off the dominant pinky; interlock, where the offhand index finger interlocks with the dominant pinky and ring finger; double or triple overlap and so on.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Recently, "cross handed" putting has become a popular trend amongst professional golfers and amateurs. Cross handed putting is the idea that the dominant hand is on top of the grip where the weak hand is on the bottom. This grip restricts the motion in your dominant hand and eliminates the possibility of wrist breakdowns through the putting stroke.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other notable putting styles include "the claw", a style that has the grip directly in between the thumb and index finger of the dominant hand while the palm faces the target.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The weak hand placed normally on the putter. Anchored putting, a style that requires a longer putter shaft that can be anchored into the players stomach or below the chin; the idea is to stabilize one end of the putter thus creating a more consistent pendulum stroke. This style will be banned in 2016 on the profession circuits.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scoring_and_handicapping">Scoring and handicapping</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Par">Par</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Par_(score)" title="Par (score)">Par (score)</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Phoenician-Canyon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/The_Phoenician-Canyon.jpg/220px-The_Phoenician-Canyon.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/The_Phoenician-Canyon.jpg/330px-The_Phoenician-Canyon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/The_Phoenician-Canyon.jpg/440px-The_Phoenician-Canyon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2590" data-file-height="1797" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Phoenician-Canyon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A par-3 hole in Phoenician Golf Club, <a href="/wiki/Scottsdale,_Arizona" title="Scottsdale, Arizona">Scottsdale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grand_Cypress_New_Course_15th_hole.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Grand_Cypress_New_Course_15th_hole.jpg/220px-Grand_Cypress_New_Course_15th_hole.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Grand_Cypress_New_Course_15th_hole.jpg/330px-Grand_Cypress_New_Course_15th_hole.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Grand_Cypress_New_Course_15th_hole.jpg/440px-Grand_Cypress_New_Course_15th_hole.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grand_Cypress_New_Course_15th_hole.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A marker stone indicating that this hole is a par-5 hole</div></div></div>\n<p>A hole is classified by its par, meaning the number of strokes a skilled golfer should require to complete play of the hole.<sup id="cite_ref-Encarta_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encarta-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The minimum par of any hole is 3 because par always includes a stroke for the tee shot and two putts.  Pars of 4 and 5 strokes are ubiquitous on golf courses; more rarely, a few courses feature par-6 and even par-7 holes. Strokes other than the tee shot and putts are expected to be made from the fairway; for example, a skilled golfer expects to reach the <a href="/wiki/Putting_green" class="mw-redirect" title="Putting green">green</a> on a par-4 hole in two strokes\xe2\x80\x94one from the tee (the "drive") and another, second, stroke to the green (the "approach")\xe2\x80\x94and then roll the ball into the hole in two putts for par. Putting the ball on the green with two strokes remaining for putts is called making "green in regulation" or GIR.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Missing a GIR does not necessarily mean a golfer will not make par, but it does make doing so more difficult as it reduces the number of putts available; conversely, making a GIR does not guarantee a par, as the player might require three or more putts to "hole out". Professional golfers typically make between 60% and 70% of greens in regulation.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The primary factor for classifying the par of a relatively straight, hazard-free hole is the distance from the tee to the green. A typical par-3 hole is less than 250 yards (225 m) in length, with a par-4 hole ranging between 251 and 475 yards (225\xe2\x80\x93434 m), and a par-5 hole being longer than 475 yards (435 m). The rare par-6s can stretch well over 650 yards (590&#160;m). These distances are based on the typical scratch golfer\'s drive distance of between 240 and 280 yards (220 and 260&#160;m); a green further than the average player\'s drive will require additional shots from the fairway. However, other considerations must be taken into account; the key question is "how many strokes would a scratch golfer take to make the green by playing along the fairway?". The grade of the land from the tee to the hole might increase or decrease the carry and rolling distance of shots as measured linearly along the ground. Sharp turns or hazards may require golfers to "lay up" on the fairway in order to change direction or hit over the hazard with their next shot. These design considerations will affect how even a scratch golfer would play the hole, irrespective of total distance from tee to green, and must be included in a determination of par.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> However, a par score never includes "expected" penalty strokes, as a scratch player is never "expected" to hit a ball into a water hazard or other unplayable situation. So, the placement of hazards only affect par when considering how a scratch golfer would avoid them.\n</p><p>Eighteen-hole courses typically total to an overall par score of 72 for a complete round; this is based on an average par of 4 for every hole, and so is often arrived at by designing a course with an equal number of par-5 and par-3 holes, the rest being par-4. Many combinations exist that total to par-72, and other course pars exist from 68 up to 76, and are not less worthy than courses of par-72. Additionally, in some countries including the United States, courses are classified according to their play difficulty, which may be used to calculate a golfer\'s playing <a href="/wiki/Handicap_(golf)" title="Handicap (golf)">handicap</a> for a given course.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The two primary difficulty ratings in the U.S. are the Course Rating, which is effectively the expected score for a zero-handicap "scratch golfer" playing the course (and may differ from the course par), and the Slope Rating, which is a measure of how much worse a "bogey golfer" (with an 18 handicap) would be expected to play than a "scratch golfer". These two numbers are available for any USGA-sanctioned course, and are used in a weighted system to calculate handicaps (see below).\n</p><p>The overall par score in a tournament is the summation of all the par scores in each round. A typical four-round professional tournament played on a par-72 course has a tournament par of 288.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scoring">Scoring</span></h4>\n<p>The goal is to play as few strokes per round as possible. A golfer\'s number of strokes in a hole, course, or tournament is compared to its respective par score, and is then reported either as the number that the golfer was "under-" or "over-par", or if it was "equal to par". A <a href="/wiki/Hole_in_one" title="Hole in one">hole in one</a> (or an "ace") occurs when a golfer sinks their ball into the cup with their first stroke from the tee. Common scores for a hole also have specific terms.<sup id="cite_ref-Encarta_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encarta-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th style="background:#dfdfdf; text-align:center;">Numeric term\n</th>\n<th style="background:#dfdfdf; text-align:center;">Name\n</th>\n<th style="background:#dfdfdf; text-align:center;">Definition\n</th></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="background:#efefef;">\xe2\x88\x924\n</td>\n<td>Condor\n</td>\n<td>four strokes under par\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="background:#efefef;">\xe2\x88\x923\n</td>\n<td>Albatross (Double Eagle)\n</td>\n<td>three strokes under par\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="background:#efefef;">\xe2\x88\x922\n</td>\n<td>Eagle\n</td>\n<td>two strokes under par\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="background:#efefef;">\xe2\x88\x921\n</td>\n<td>Birdie\n</td>\n<td>one stroke under par\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="background:#efefef;">E\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Par_(score)" title="Par (score)">Par</a>\n</td>\n<td>equal to par\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="background:#efefef;">+1\n</td>\n<td>Bogey\n</td>\n<td>one stroke over par\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="background:#efefef;">+2\n</td>\n<td>Double bogey\n</td>\n<td>two strokes over par\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="background:#efefef;">+3\n</td>\n<td>Triple bogey\n</td>\n<td>three strokes over par\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In a typical professional tournament or among "scratch" amateur players, "birdie-bogey" play is common; a player will "lose" a stroke by bogeying a hole, then "gain" one by scoring a birdie. Eagles are uncommon but not rare; however, only <a href="/wiki/List_of_albatrosses_in_important_tournaments#Major_championships" title="List of albatrosses in important tournaments">18 players have scored an albatross</a> in a men\'s major championship.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Basic_forms_of_golf">Basic forms of golf</span></h3>\n<p>There are two basic forms of golf play, match play and stroke play. Stroke play is more popular.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Match_play">Match play</span></h4>\n<p>Two players (or two teams) play each hole as a separate contest against each other in what is called <a href="/wiki/Match_play" title="Match play">match play</a>. The party with the lower score wins that hole, or if the scores of both players or teams are equal the hole is "halved" (or tied). The game is won by the party that wins more holes than the other. In the case that one team or player has taken a lead that cannot be overcome in the number of holes remaining to be played, the match is deemed to be won by the party in the lead, and the remainder of the holes are not played. For example, if one party already has a lead of six holes, and only five holes remain to be played on the course, the match is over and the winning party is deemed to have won "6 &amp; 5". At any given point, if the lead is equal to the number of holes remaining, the party leading the match is said to be "dormie", and the match is continued until the party increases the lead by one hole or ties any of the remaining holes, thereby winning the match, or until the match ends in a tie with the lead player\'s opponent winning all remaining holes. When the game is tied after the predetermined number of holes have been played, it may be continued until one side takes a one-hole lead.<sup id="cite_ref-Encarta_16-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encarta-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stroke_play">Stroke play</span></h4>\n<p>The score achieved for each and every hole of the round or tournament is added to produce the total score, and the player with the lowest score wins in <a href="/wiki/Stroke_play" title="Stroke play">stroke play</a>. Stroke play is the game most commonly played by professional golfers. If there is a tie after the regulation number of holes in a professional tournament, a playoff takes place between all tied players. Playoffs either are sudden death or employ a pre-determined number of holes, anywhere from three to a full 18. In sudden death, a player who scores lower on a hole than all of his opponents wins the match. If at least two players remain tied after such a playoff using a pre-determined number of holes, then play continues in sudden death format, where the first player to win a hole wins the tournament.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_forms_of_play">Other forms of play</span></h3>\n<p>The other forms of play in the game of golf are bogey competition, skins, 9-points, stableford, team play, and unofficial team variations.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bogey_competition">Bogey competition</span></h4>\n<p>A bogey competition is a scoring format sometimes seen in at informal tournaments. Its scoring is similar to match play, except each player compares their hole score to the hole\'s par rating instead of the score of another player. The player "wins" the hole if they score a birdie or better, they "lose" the hole if they score a bogey or worse, and they "halve" the hole by scoring par. By recording only this simple win-loss-halve score on the sheet, a player can shrug off a very poorly-played hole with a simple "-" mark and move on. As used in competitions, the player or pair with the best win-loss "differential" wins the competition.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Skins">Skins</span></h4>\n<p>What\'s known as the <a href="/wiki/Skins_Game" class="mw-redirect" title="Skins Game">skins game</a> is a variation on the match play where each hole has an amount of money (called "skin") attached to it. The lump sum may be prize money at the professional level (the most famous event to use these rules was the "<a href="/wiki/LG_Skins_Game" class="mw-redirect" title="LG Skins Game">LG Skins Game</a>", played at <a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Wells_Golf_Resort&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Indian Wells Golf Resort (page does not exist)">Indian Wells Golf Resort</a> in California until 2008), or an amount wagered for each hole among amateur players. The player with the lowest score on the hole wins the skin for that hole; if two or more players tie for the lowest score, the skin carries over to the next hole. The game continues until a player wins a hole outright, which may (and evidently often does) result in a player receiving money for a previous hole that they had not tied for.\n</p><p>If players tie the 18th hole, either all players or only the tying players repeat the 18th hole until an outright winner is decided for that hole\xe2\x80\x94and all undecided skins.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="9-Points">9-Points</span></h4>\n<p>A nine-point game is another variant of match play typically played among threesomes, where each hole is worth a total of nine points. The player with the lowest score on a hole receives five points, the next-lowest score 3 and the next-lowest score 1. Ties are generally resolved by summing the points contested and dividing them among the tying players; a two-way tie for first is worth four points to both players, a two-way tie for second is worth two points to both players, and a three-way tie is worth three points to each player. The player with the highest score after 18 holes (in which there are 162 points to be awarded) wins the game. This format can be used to wager on the game systematically; players each contribute the same amount of money to the pot, and a dollar value is assigned to each point scored (or each point after 18) based on the amount of money in the pot, with any overage going to the overall winner.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stableford">Stableford</span></h4>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Stableford" title="Stableford">Stableford</a> system is a simplification of stroke play that awards players points based on their score relative to the hole\'s par; the score for a hole is calculated by taking the par score, adding 2, then subtracting the player\'s hole score, making the result zero if negative. Alternately stated, a double bogey or worse is zero points, a bogey is worth one point, par is two, a birdie three, an eagle four, and so on. The advantages of this system over stroke play are a more natural "higher is better" scoring, the ability to compare Stableford scores between plays on courses with different total par scores (scoring an "even" in stroke play will always give a Stableford score of 36), discouraging the tendency to abandon the entire game after playing a particularly bad hole (a novice playing by strict rules may score as high as an 8 or 10 on a single difficult hole; their Stableford score for the hole would be zero, which puts them only two points behind par no matter how badly they played), and the ability to simply pick up one\'s ball once it is impossible to score any points for the hole, which speeds play.\n</p><p>The USGA and R&amp;A sanction a "Modified Stableford" system for scratch players, which makes par worth zero, a birdie worth 2, eagle 5 and double-eagle 8, while a bogey is a penalty of \xe2\x88\x921 and a double-bogey or worse \xe2\x88\x923. As with the original system, the highest score wins the game, and terrible scores on one or two holes won\'t wreck an entire game, but this system rewards "bogey-birdie" play more than the original, encouraging golfers to try to make the riskier birdie putt or eagle chipshot instead of simply parring each hole.<sup id="cite_ref-Encarta_16-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encarta-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Team_play">Team play</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jun%C3%ADn_Golf_Club_20101031_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Jun%C3%ADn_Golf_Club_20101031_001.jpg/220px-Jun%C3%ADn_Golf_Club_20101031_001.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Jun%C3%ADn_Golf_Club_20101031_001.jpg/330px-Jun%C3%ADn_Golf_Club_20101031_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Jun%C3%ADn_Golf_Club_20101031_001.jpg/440px-Jun%C3%ADn_Golf_Club_20101031_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jun%C3%ADn_Golf_Club_20101031_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jun\xc3\xadn Golf Club, in <a href="/wiki/Jun%C3%ADn,_Buenos_Aires" title="Jun\xc3\xadn, Buenos Aires">Jun\xc3\xadn</a>, Argentina</div></div></div>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Foursome_(golf)" title="Foursome (golf)">Foursome</a>: defined in Rule 29, this is played between two teams of two players each, in which each team has only one ball and players alternate playing it. For example, if players "A" and "B" form a team, "A" tees off on the first hole, "B" will play the second shot, "A" the third, and so on until the hole is finished. On the second hole, "B" will tee off (regardless who played the last putt on the first hole), then "A" plays the second shot, and so on. Foursomes can be played as match play or stroke play.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Four-ball_golf" title="Four-ball golf">Fourball</a>: defined in Rules 30 and 31, this is also played between two teams of two players each, but every player plays their own ball and for each team, the lower score on each hole counts. Fourballs can be played as match play or stroke play.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Unofficial_team_variations">Unofficial team variations</span></h5>\n<ul><li>Scramble: also known as ambrose or best-shot; each player in a team tees off on each hole, and the players decide which shot was best. Every player then plays their second shot from within a clublength of where the best shot has come to rest (and no closer to the hole), and the procedure is repeated until the hole is finished. This system is very common at informal tournaments such as for charity, as it speeds play (due to the reduced number of shots taken from bad lies), allows teams of varying sizes, and allows players of widely varying skill levels to participate without profoundly affecting team score.</li>\n<li>Champagne scramble: a combination of a scramble and best-ball, only the first shot of each hole is a scramble; all players tee off, decide on the best tee shot, then each player plays their own ball starting at that point until they hole out, without deciding any further "best shots". The best score amongst the team\'s players is counted.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Better ball or best-ball: like fourball, each player plays the hole as normal, but the lowest score of all the players on the team counts as the team\'s score for the hole.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Greensome (also known as Scotch Foursomes): also called modified alternate shot, this is played in pairs; both players tee off, and then pick the best shot as in a scramble. The player who did not shoot the best first shot plays the second shot. The play then alternates as in a foursome.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> A variant of greensome is sometimes played where the opposing team chooses which of their opponent\'s tee shots the opponents should use. The player who did not shoot the chosen first shot plays the second shot. Play then continues as a greensome.</li>\n<li>Wolf (also known as Ship, Captain &amp; Crew, Captain, Pig): a version of match play; with a foursome an order of play for each player is established for the duration of the round.  The first player hits a ball from the tee, then waits for each successive player to hit (2nd, 3rd and 4th).  After each player hits the 1st player has the option of choosing a partner for the hole (the 1st player is the Wolf for that hole) usually by calling Wolf before the next player hits.  Once a partner is picked, each two-some (the Wolf and his or her partner vs the remaining two players) scores their total strokes and the winning two-some is awarded 1-point each for winning a hole and zero points for tying.  The next hole, the rotation moves forward (e.g. the 2nd player is now hitting 1st and the Wolf and the previous Wolf hits last). A Wolf can decide to go alone to win extra points, but they must beat all other players in stroke play on that hole.  If alone, the Wolf is awarded 2-points for going alone after everyone has hit or 4 points for declaring Lone Wolf before anyone else hits.  If the Lone Wolf loses, to even one player, the 3 other players get 1-point each. The winner is the player with the most points at the end of the round.  Strategically, care must be taken not to let a low-handicap player run away with all the points by being constantly paired with the Wolf.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup>,</sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Shotgun_start" title="Shotgun start">Shotgun starts</a> are mainly used for amateur tournament play. In this variant, each of the groups playing starts their game on a different hole, allowing for all players to start and end their round at roughly the same time. All 18 holes are still played, but a player or foursome may, for instance, start on hole 5, play through to the 18th hole, then continue with hole 1 and end on hole 4. This speeds the completion of the entire event as players are not kept waiting for progressive tee times at the first hole. This form of play, as a minor variation to stroke or match play, is neither defined nor disallowed by strict rules and so is used according to local rules for an event.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Handicap_systems">Handicap systems</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Handicap_(golf)" title="Handicap (golf)">Handicap (golf)</a></div>\n<p>A handicap is a numerical measure of an amateur golfer\'s ability to play golf over the course of 18 holes. A player\'s handicap generally represents the number of strokes above par that the player will make over the course of an above-average round of golf. The better the player the lower their handicap is. Someone with a handicap of 0 or less is often called a <i>scratch golfer</i>, and would typically score or beat the course par on a round of play (depending on course difficulty).\n</p><p>Calculating a handicap is often complicated, the general reason being that golf courses are not uniformly challenging from course to course or between skill levels. A player scoring even par on Course A might average four over par on course B, while a player averaging 20 over par on course A might average only 16 over on course B. So, to the "scratch golfer", Course B is more difficult, but to the "bogey golfer", Course A is more difficult. The reasons for this are inherent in the types of challenges presented by the same course to both golfers. Distance is often a problem for amateur "bogey" golfers with slower swing speeds, who get less distance with each club, and so typically require more shots to get to the green, raising their score compared to a scratch golfer with a stronger swing. However, courses are often designed with hazard placement to mitigate this advantage, forcing the scratch player to "lay up" to avoid bunkers or water, while the bogey golfer is more or less unaffected as the hazard lies out of their range. Finally, terrain features and fairway maintenance can affect golfers of all skill levels; narrowing the fairway by adding obstacles or widening the rough on each side will typically increase the percentage of shots made from disadvantageous lies, increasing the challenge for all players.\n</p><p>By USGA rules, handicap calculation first requires calculating a "Handicap Differential" for each round of play the player has completed by strict rules. That in itself is a function of the player\'s "gross adjusted score" (adjustments can be made to mitigate various deviations either from strict rules or from a player\'s normal capabilities, for handicap purposes only) and two course-specific difficulty ratings: the Course Rating, a calculated expected score for a hypothetical "scratch golfer": and the Slope Rating, a number based on how much worse a hypothetical 20-handicap "bogey golfer" would score compared to the "scratch golfer". The average Slope Rating of all USGA-rated courses as of 2012 is 113, which also factors into the Differential computation.\n</p><p>The most recent Differentials are logged, up to 20 of them, and then the best of these (the number used depends on the number available) are selected, averaged, multiplied by .96 (an "excellence factor" that reduces the handicap of higher-scoring players, encouraging them to play better and thus lower their handicap), and truncated to the tenths place to produce the "Handicap Index". Additional calculations can be used to place higher significance on a player\'s recent tournament scores. A player\'s Handicap Index is then multiplied by the Slope Rating of the course to be played, divided by the average Slope Rating of 113, then rounded to the nearest integer to produce the player\'s Course Handicap.\n</p><p>Once calculated, the Course Handicap is applied in stroke play by simply reducing the player\'s gross score by the handicap, to produce a net score. So, a gross score of 96 with a handicap of 22 would produce a net score of 74. In match play, the lower handicap is subtracted from the higher handicap, and the resulting handicap strokes are awarded to the higher handicapper by distributing them among the holes according to each hole\'s difficulty; holes are ranked on the scorecard from 1 to 18 (or however many holes are available), and one stroke is applied to each hole from the most difficult to the least difficult. So, if one player has a 9 handicap and another has a 25 handicap, the 25-handicap player receives one handicap stroke on each of the most difficult 16 holes (25-9). If the 25-handicapper were playing against a "scratch golfer" (zero handicap), all 25 strokes would be distributed, first by applying one stroke to each hole, then applying the remaining strokes, one each, to the most difficult 7 holes; so, the handicap player would subtract 2 strokes from each of the most difficult 7 holes, and 1 each from the remaining 11.\n</p><p>Handicap systems have potential for abuse by players who may intentionally play badly to increase their handicap ("throwing their \'cap") before playing to their potential at an important event with a valuable prize. For this reason, professional golf associations do not use them, but they can be calculated and used along with other criteria to determine the relative strengths of various professional players. Touring professionals, being the best of the best, often have negative handicaps; they can be expected, on average, to score lower than the Course Rating on any course.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Popularity">Popularity</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Golfveld.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Golfveld.jpg/220px-Golfveld.jpg" width="220" height="118" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Golfveld.jpg/330px-Golfveld.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Golfveld.jpg/440px-Golfveld.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1098" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Golfveld.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Part of a golf course in western India</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Golf_course,_Italy_-_20070620.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Golf_course%2C_Italy_-_20070620.jpg/220px-Golf_course%2C_Italy_-_20070620.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Golf_course%2C_Italy_-_20070620.jpg/330px-Golf_course%2C_Italy_-_20070620.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Golf_course%2C_Italy_-_20070620.jpg/440px-Golf_course%2C_Italy_-_20070620.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Golf_course,_Italy_-_20070620.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An aerial view of a golf course in Italy</div></div></div>\n<p>In 2005 <i><a href="/wiki/Golf_Digest" title="Golf Digest">Golf Digest</a></i> calculated that the countries with most golf courses per capita, in order, were: Scotland, New Zealand, Australia, Ireland, Canada, Wales, United States, Sweden, and England (countries with fewer than 500,000 people were excluded).\n</p><p>The number of courses in other territories has increased, an example of this being the expansion of <a href="/wiki/Golf_in_China" title="Golf in China">golf in China</a>. The first golf course in China opened in 1984, but by the end of 2009 there were roughly 600 in the country. For much of the 21st century, development of new golf courses in China has been officially banned (with the exception of the island province of <a href="/wiki/Hainan" title="Hainan">Hainan</a>), but the number of courses had nonetheless tripled from 2004 to 2009; the "ban" has been evaded with the government\'s tacit approval simply by not mentioning golf in any development plans.<sup id="cite_ref-China_golf_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-China_golf-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the United States, the number of people who play golf twenty-five times or more per year decreased from 6.9&#160;million in 2000 to 4.6&#160;million in 2005,<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> according to the <a href="/wiki/National_Golf_Foundation" title="National Golf Foundation">National Golf Foundation</a>. The NGF reported that the number who played golf at all decreased from 30 to 26 million over the same period.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In February 1971, <a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">astronaut</a> <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shepard" title="Alan Shepard">Alan Shepard</a> became the first person to golf anywhere other than <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. He smuggled a golf club and two golf balls on board <a href="/wiki/Apollo_14" title="Apollo 14">Apollo 14</a> with the intent to golf on the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. He attempted two drives. He shanked the first attempt, but it is estimated his second went more than 200 <a href="/wiki/Yards" class="mw-redirect" title="Yards">yards</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Golf_courses_worldwide">Golf courses worldwide</span></h3>\n<p>Number of golf courses by country in 2015. Below are the top 18 countries that have the most golf courses.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table Korea\n</td>\n<td>447\n</td>\n<td>1%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Spain\n</td>\n<td>437\n</td>\n<td>1%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>New
